  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Normalization bottlenecks in high-precision reductions lag behind gains in matrix-multiplication performance; reducing reduction work can yield practical speedups in DL training.

Method: Propose MXNorm, estimating RMS from block scales produced by MXFP8 cast to enable a 32x reduction in reduction size; validate on pre-training Llama 3 models (125M, 1B, 8B); compare to RMSNorm with MXFP8 matmuls; report kernel speedups using torch.compile.

Result: Minimal loss of training accuracy vs. RMSNorm baseline; kernel speedups up to 2.4x with MXNorm over RMSNorm; corresponding layer-level improvements of 1.3% (MXFP8) and 2.6% (NVFP4).

Conclusion: MXNorm offers a practical, drop-in normalization acceleration compatible with MXFP8/NVFP4 stacks, delivering significant throughput gains for large-scale pretraining with little to no accuracy cost.

Abstract: Matrix multiplication performance has long been the major bottleneck to scaling deep learning workloads, which has stimulated the design of new accelerators that use increasingly low-precision number formats. However, improvements in matrix multiplication performance have far outstripped improvements in performance on reductions and elementwise computations, which are still being performed in higher precision. In this work, we propose MXNorm, a drop-in replacement for RMSNorm that estimates the RMS using only the block scales calculated as part of the MXFP8 cast and enables a 32x decrease in the size of reduction needed for normalization. We validate our approximation method on pre-training of Llama 3 models of 125M, 1B and 8B parameters, finding minimal loss of training accuracy compared to a baseline using RMSNorm with MXFP8 matmuls. We also show practical kernel speedups using only torch.compile of up to 2.4x for MXNorm over RMSNorm, corresponding to a 1.3% speedup in Llama 3 8B transformer layers in MXFP8 and a 2.6% speedup in NVFP4.

</details>


### [245] [Learnability and Privacy Vulnerability are Entangled in a Few Critical Weights](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13186)
*Xingli Fang,Jung-Eun Kim*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A weight-level privacy approach that identifies a tiny subset of 


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing membership privacy methods retrain all weights, causing cost and utility loss. The paper seeks to reduce both by focusing on a small set of critical weights that influence utility and privacy, leveraging the observation that location matters more than value.

Method: Score critical weights by location; avoid discarding neurons. Instead, rewind only the critical weights for fine-tuning to improve privacy against membership inference attacks while preserving utility.

Result: Empirical results on various models/datasets show improved resistance to Membership Inference Attacks with maintained utility compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Targeted, weight-level privacy control can offer better privacy-utility trade-offs by focusing on weight locations and selectively rewinding rather than full retraining.

Abstract: Prior approaches for membership privacy preservation usually update or retrain all weights in neural networks, which is costly and can lead to unnecessary utility loss or even more serious misalignment in predictions between training data and non-training data. In this work, we observed three insights: i) privacy vulnerability exists in a very small fraction of weights; ii) however, most of those weights also critically impact utility performance; iii) the importance of weights stems from their locations rather than their values. According to these insights, to preserve privacy, we score critical weights, and instead of discarding those neurons, we rewind only the weights for fine-tuning. We show that, through extensive experiments, this mechanism exhibits outperforming resilience in most cases against Membership Inference Attacks while maintaining utility.

</details>


### [246] [Representation Learning for Spatiotemporal Physical Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.13227)
*Helen Qu,Rudy Morel,Michael McCabe,Alberto Bietti,François Lanusse,Shirley Ho,Yann LeCun*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Latent-space self-supervised representations (e.g., JEPAs) yield better downstream physics-task performance than pixel-level predictions or some physics-modeling approaches in spatiotemporal systems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To evaluate whether general-purpose self-supervised learning can produce physics-relevant representations useful for downstream tasks (e.g., estimating governing parameters) and to compare with specialized physics-based modeling methods.

Method: Empirical evaluation of various self-supervised methods on spatiotemporal data, comparing latent-space objectives (e.g., joint embedding predictive architectures) against pixel-level prediction objectives, and against physics-grounded baselines, evaluating their effectiveness on downstream tasks like parameter estimation.

Result: Not all physics-modeling methods outperform generic SSL; latent-space methods (JEPAs) outperform pixel-level prediction objectives; downstream task performance improves with latent representations.

Conclusion: Focusing on latent-space self-supervision yields representations more relevant for downstream physical tasks than pixel-wise predictive objectives, suggesting a shift away from next-frame prediction as the primary objective in physics-guided representation learning; code available.

Abstract: Machine learning approaches to spatiotemporal physical systems have primarily focused on next-frame prediction, with the goal of learning an accurate emulator for the system's evolution in time. However, these emulators are computationally expensive to train and are subject to performance pitfalls, such as compounding errors during autoregressive rollout. In this work, we take a different perspective and look at scientific tasks further downstream of predicting the next frame, such as estimation of a system's governing physical parameters. Accuracy on these tasks offers a uniquely quantifiable glimpse into the physical relevance of the representations of these models. We evaluate the effectiveness of general-purpose self-supervised methods in learning physics-grounded representations that are useful for downstream scientific tasks. Surprisingly, we find that not all methods designed for physical modeling outperform generic self-supervised learning methods on these tasks, and methods that learn in the latent space (e.g., joint embedding predictive architectures, or JEPAs) outperform those optimizing pixel-level prediction objectives. Code is available at https://github.com/helenqu/physical-representation-learning.

</details>
